  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5a4213eb-15c1-4a32-a838-2c5b3a9d02b3'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer2456326033906612892">Since its first publication in 1965, this edition has been widely hailed as the best available text of Blake's poetry and prose. <span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This probably isn't even relevant to you now but sadly, this book does not combine his artistic work. <br/><br/>However, I highly recommend visiting The Blake A<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This probably isn't even relevant to you now but sadly, this book does not combine his artistic work. <br /><br />However, I highly recommend visiting The Blake Archive online. It's free to use and you can see a vast amount of his work in high-resolution images. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2006890420" />
    <a title="Hussam Elkhatib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66942714-hussam-elkhatib"><img alt="Hussam Elkhatib" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492664243p2/66942714.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2006890420?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hussam Elkhatib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hussam Elkhatib" href="/user/show/66942714-hussam-elkhatib">Hussam Elkhatib</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2006890420" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12430759300505363647">William Blake is one of the greatest poets of the English language. I'd recommend this book to anyone interested in the mysteries of life. A great concept that William Blake shared was how the individual ought to draw his or hers own conclusions and find their personalized ways, otherwise, they'll become a slave to someone else's thoughts the rest of their lives. Very well worth the read. Furthermore, anyone who's interested in republicanism and democracy should put this great book on their shel</span>
  <span id="freeText12430759300505363647" style="display:none">William Blake is one of the greatest poets of the English language. I'd recommend this book to anyone interested in the mysteries of life. A great concept that William Blake shared was how the individual ought to draw his or hers own conclusions and find their personalized ways, otherwise, they'll become a slave to someone else's thoughts the rest of their lives. Very well worth the read. Furthermore, anyone who's interested in republicanism and democracy should put this great book on their shelf. I thoroughly enjoyed both the poetry and prose in it. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12430759300505363647" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Alins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4244557-alins"><img alt="Alins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291743082p2/4244557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/134006659?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alins" href="/user/show/4244557-alins">Alins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer134006659&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer134006659" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer481466036660733318">How is it possible that Blake was able to articulate the full enormity of his vision while keeping up with the complex mythological narrative he invented? Through much of his longer poetical works he so effortlessly associates his mammoth cosmic figures with a riveting and impassioned philosophical discourse. One wonders whether the man was even human. And yet it is precisely this disbelief he and, a bit later and with more severity, Nietzsche preached against.<br/><br/>For Blake, the human imagination is</span>
  <span id="freeText481466036660733318" style="display:none">How is it possible that Blake was able to articulate the full enormity of his vision while keeping up with the complex mythological narrative he invented? Through much of his longer poetical works he so effortlessly associates his mammoth cosmic figures with a riveting and impassioned philosophical discourse. One wonders whether the man was even human. And yet it is precisely this disbelief he and, a bit later and with more severity, Nietzsche preached against.<br /><br />For Blake, the human imagination is the gateway to eternity, and anyone is capable of it. Yet even this vote of confidence in man falls short of Nietzsche's often misunderstood optimism when he asserts that artistic achievement has nothing to do with the "muse" and is wholly within the boundaries of human ability. For Nietzsche, man alone responsible for his great works, and to credit anything else with their sublimity is to undermine human potential. But Blake, a man of profound faith, contends that "When I am commanded by the spirits, then I write; and the moment I have written, I see the words fly about the room in all directions." Yet how telling it is that even this humble explaining-away of his powers is fraught with poetry.<br /><br />But whether or not Blake credited himself with his evident genius, the works speak for themselves. And for "The Complete Poetry and Prose," two of the most competent Blake scholars join forces to offer a clear, extensive and informed presentation of Blake's output. Astonishing in scope and scholarship, this Erdman/Bloom edition is as indispensable for Blake's work as it is for their notes and commentary. My only complaint is the inconsistency of Bloom's commentary. He skips entire sections of Blake's worth here, whereas he covers every last word of it in his own book, "Blake's Apocalypse." Hmm . . . Harold wouldn't, by any chance, be trying to get us to, um, SPEND a little more, you think? Naaahhh.<br /><br />But no commentary -- no matter how illuminating -- will ever approach the radiance of Blake's own words. From the plainspoken awe of the early masterpieces, "Ahania" and "The Book of Thel," to the astonishing epics "The Four Zoas" and "Jerusalem" and right down to "The Mental Traveler," that late fruition of Blake's bitingly ironical voice, "The Complete Poems and Prose" reads like a Bible in its own right. Routinely taking on such lofty subjects as the fall of man, love and jealousy, desire and reason, good and evil, a reading of Blake's work all together yields just as much beauty, mystery and genius.<br /><br />Equally as riveting are the many letters with which the primary portion of the book concludes. Rife with the fierce inquisitiveness and confidence that characterizes the famous "Proverbs of Hell," Blake's letters rank with those of Keats and Hopkins as some of the most stirring autobiographical sketches ever produced by a master poet.<br /><br />Erdman's "Prophet Against Empire" and Bloom's aforementioned "Blake's Apocalypse" make for great companion pieces, as does Frye's "Fearful Symmetry." But I think the best way for new readers to take on Blake's work is by diving blindly into its initially cold waters, and only calling in Erdman, Bloom and Frye to turn up the heat later on.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="481466036660733318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7591727477960507658">Ladies and gentlemen, make way for the BLOWOUT! scholarly edition of the man who kept me focused on the inherently fated and apocalyptic nature of modern life for a few weeks. I've been a fan ever since high school, and felt the need to clean house and read the complete works, including the really boring, ephemeral letters.   <span id="freeText7591727477960507658" style="display:none">Ladies and gentlemen, make way for the BLOWOUT! scholarly edition of the man who kept me focused on the inherently fated and apocalyptic nature of modern life for a few weeks. I've been a fan ever since high school, and felt the need to clean house and read the complete works, including the really boring, ephemeral letters. With this, you get a complete picture of Blake's worldview, and the bizarre mythological systems he dreamt up to convey his vision of life in England around the turn of the 19th Century, dark Satanic mills and all.</span>

<div id="review_68393501" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/68393501" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2652905-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1250918430p2/2652905.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/68393501?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/2652905-lisa">Lisa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer68393501" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15913251078264018311">William Blake became one of the most highly regarded writers and painters during his time--after his death. He was opinionated and luckily he was afforded the opportunity to express views that others were persecuted for expressing during that time in history. To fully understand his work, study his life and the societal norms of the day. Otherwise you can't fully appreciate the beauty of his ideas.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_17673363" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17673363" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/929972-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267461501p2/929972.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17673363?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/929972-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780520044739" href="/book/show/23909.The_Complete_Poetry_and_Prose_of_William_Blake_New_and_Revised_edition">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/929972-dan?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17673363" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3690042981859381292">(Review edited July 2016)<br/><br/>While frequently described as "pre-Romantic," Blake wrote on many themes typically associated with Romanticism, including nature, imagination and the experiences of childhood. One significant way in which Blake differs from the Romantic poets, however, is in his use of myth. While poets like <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11978.John_Keats' title='John Keats' rel='nofollow noopener'>Keats</a> or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/45882.Percy_Bysshe_Shelley' title='Percy Bysshe Shelley' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shelley</a> might make reference to a recognized character from classical myth (even basing a longer work on such a character, as in the case of Shelley's verse drama <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80577.Prometheus_Unbound' title='Prometheus Unbound by Percy Bysshe Shelley' rel='nofollow noopener'>Prometheus</a></span>
  <span id="freeText3690042981859381292" style="display:none">(Review edited July 2016)<br /><br />While frequently described as "pre-Romantic," Blake wrote on many themes typically associated with Romanticism, including nature, imagination and the experiences of childhood. One significant way in which Blake differs from the Romantic poets, however, is in his use of myth. While poets like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11978.John_Keats" title="John Keats" rel="nofollow noopener">Keats</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/45882.Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener">Shelley</a> might make reference to a recognized character from classical myth (even basing a longer work on such a character, as in the case of Shelley's verse drama <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80577.Prometheus_Unbound" title="Prometheus Unbound by Percy Bysshe Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener">Prometheus Unbound</a>), Blake's mythical references are to such obscure beings as Urizen, Luvah and Tharmas, and to mysterious entities such as spectres and emanations. These characters are not from any earlier mythic tradition, but were invented by Blake in his major “prophetic” works, including <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7497447.The_Four_Zoas" title="The Four Zoas by William Blake" rel="nofollow noopener">The Four Zoas</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1168149.Milton" title="Milton by William Blake" rel="nofollow noopener">Milton</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13531504.Jerusalem_A_Cookbook" title="Jerusalem A Cookbook by Yotam Ottolenghi" rel="nofollow noopener">Jerusalem</a>. In the context of his constructing an original mythology, I think my favorite quote by Blake has to be "I must Create a System or be enslav'd by another Mans; I will not Reason &amp; Compare: my business is to Create." As a mythopoeic artist, if Blake is to be compared to anyone, perhaps it should be to writers like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/656983.J_R_R__Tolkien" title="J.R.R. Tolkien" rel="nofollow noopener">J.R. Tolkien</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16517863.H_P_Lovecraft" title="H.P Lovecraft" rel="nofollow noopener">H P Lovecraft</a>.<br /><br />(For me, Blake's major prophetic works rank up there along with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11013.Finnegans_Wake" title="Finnegans Wake by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Finnegans Wake</a> with regard to the amount of intellectual effort one must make in order to make sense of what is going on at any point in the work. I have found <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/58765.Northrop_Frye" title="Northrop Frye" rel="nofollow noopener">Northrop Frye</a>’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1491229.Fearful_Symmetry_A_Study_of_William_Blake" title="Fearful Symmetry A Study of William Blake by Northrop Frye" rel="nofollow noopener">Fearful Symmetry: A Study of William Blake</a> useful in its commentary on the general outlines of Blake's mythological system).<br /><br />Along with the major and minor prophecies are included Blake's earlier and less daunting works such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/171547.Songs_of_Innocence_and_of_Experience" title="Songs of Innocence and of Experience by William Blake" rel="nofollow noopener">Songs of Innocence And of Experience</a> (but while these lyrical poems are much more accessible, they, too,have their ambiguities—on which latter I enjoy <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/236.Harold_Bloom" title="Harold Bloom" rel="nofollow noopener">Harold Bloom</a>'s commentary, particularly in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2251079.Blake_s_Apocalypse_A_Study_in_Poetic_Argument" title="Blake's Apocalypse A Study in Poetic Argument by Harold Bloom" rel="nofollow noopener">Blake's Apocalypse: A Study in Poetic Argument</a>).<br /><br />Other works like <i>An Island in the Moon</i> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23913.The_Marriage_of_Heaven_and_Hell" title="The Marriage of Heaven and Hell by William Blake" rel="nofollow noopener">The Marriage of Heaven and Hell</a> do not require familiarity with Blake's mythology. For its paradoxicality, and particularly for its "Proverbs of Hell," which reminds me other aphoristic writers like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1938.Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche" rel="nofollow noopener">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/77989.Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus" rel="nofollow noopener">Heraclitus</a>, <i>The Marriage</i> is a particular favorite of mine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3690042981859381292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_68871917" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer10735091142361486928">Blake was a printer and published his own poems—many illustrated. In fact scholars debate which pantheon to place him: artist or poet. Because of the labor intensive and time consuming manner in which type was set my hand, Blake’s poetry is extremely economic. It is this conservation of words expressing such an abundance of ideas that makes his writing profoundly simple. That combined with the fact he had visions and was probably insane makes for great poetry.<br/><br/>"Hear the voice of the Bard!<br/>Who Pres</span>
  <span id="freeText10735091142361486928" style="display:none">Blake was a printer and published his own poems—many illustrated. In fact scholars debate which pantheon to place him: artist or poet. Because of the labor intensive and time consuming manner in which type was set my hand, Blake’s poetry is extremely economic. It is this conservation of words expressing such an abundance of ideas that makes his writing profoundly simple. That combined with the fact he had visions and was probably insane makes for great poetry.<br /><br />"Hear the voice of the Bard!<br />Who Present, Past, &amp; Future sees<br />Whose ears have heard<br />The Holy Word,<br />That walk’d among the ancient trees.<br /><br />Calling the lapsed Soul<br />And weeping in the evening dew;<br />That might controll.<br />The starry pole;<br />And fallen fallen light renew!<br /><br />O Earth O Earth return!<br />Arise from out the dewy grass;<br />Night is worn,<br />And the morn<br />Rises from the slumbrous mass.<br /><br />Turn away no more:<br />Why wilt thou turn away<br />The starry floor<br />The watery shore<br />Is given thee till the break of day."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10735091142361486928" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11080905963621203255">Erdman's edition of the complete poetry and prose is now the standard edition of Blake's complete works and is the edition referenced by the Blake concordance online (as well as being available online, but Bloom's commentary is not).   <span id="freeText11080905963621203255" style="display:none">Erdman's edition of the complete poetry and prose is now the standard edition of Blake's complete works and is the edition referenced by the Blake concordance online (as well as being available online, but Bloom's commentary is not). It is superior editorially to Keynes's edition, but the organization of Keynes's edition always made more sense to me, being more strictly chronological. Furthermore, the Erdman edition heavily edits The Four Zoas to make it a coherent text, so I recommend direct study of the manuscript of FZ to those serious about it, making reference to Erdman's version of FZ as one reading of the poem rather than the poem itself.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780192810502" href="/book/show/478556.Complete_Writings_with_Variant_Readings">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1099401682" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15249877180220254031">It's hard to think of another poet who has had so much influence on modern speculative fiction. Lyrical brilliance combined with a boundless imagination and, as you'll discover from his correspondence and more ribald verse, a profound sense of humour. However, it's in Blake's contemplations of the spiritual and metaphysical that most fellow fantasists will find greatest inspiration: 'To see a world in a grain of sand...' Try beating that one.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_514954057" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/514954057" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4471663-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507045708p2/4471663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/514954057?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/4471663-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer514954057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3566791445345741909">I borrowed The Complete Poetry & Prose of William Blake through the Arrowhead interlibrary loan system, which serves northeast Minnesota. I must return it by Feb 4, 2013. Literally, it is a very heavy book in paperback. You could prop up an end of a bookshelf with it, or use it as a doorstop. <br/><br/>I like it so much, I may buy a copy. I knew Blake composed beautiful work. I knew little about how radical he was for his time. He created most of his work during the period from 1784 until his death in 182</span>
  <span id="freeText3566791445345741909" style="display:none">I borrowed The Complete Poetry &amp; Prose of William Blake through the Arrowhead interlibrary loan system, which serves northeast Minnesota. I must return it by Feb 4, 2013. Literally, it is a very heavy book in paperback. You could prop up an end of a bookshelf with it, or use it as a doorstop. <br /><br />I like it so much, I may buy a copy. I knew Blake composed beautiful work. I knew little about how radical he was for his time. He created most of his work during the period from 1784 until his death in 1827. He challenges whatever you believe, whether traditional Christian or Humanist, and often writes his own opposing opinions back-to-back. He challenged the translations of Greek and Roman mythology, from Chaucer, Marlowe, and Milton. He has little good to say about the Church of England, or Rome. I thought he was a follower of Swedenborg, but he is an antagonist, and rewrites the doctrines. <br /><br />The Commentary by Harold Bloom, 80 pages of it at the back of the book, is an interesting companion to the text. You do not have to read the book in sequence. At least read "The Visions of the Daughters of Albion" (the hymn to free love), and "The Book of Urizen", a creation story that never ends.<br /><br />"Tell me what is day or night to one o'erflowd with woe?<br />Tell me what is a thought? &amp; of what substance is it made?<br />Tell me what is joy? &amp; in what garden do joys grow?<br />And in what river swim the sorrows? and upon what mountains<br />Wave shadows of discontent? and in what houses dwell the wretched<br />Drunken with woe forgotten, and shut up from cold despair.<br /><br />Tell me where dwell the thoughts forgotten till thou call them forth<br />Tell me where dwell the joys of old! &amp; where the ancient loves?<br />And when will they renew again &amp; the night of oblivion past? ..." pp. 47-8<br /><br />"I cry, Love! Love! Love! happy happy Love! free as the mountain wind!" p. 50<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3566791445345741909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_29524301" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/29524301" />
    <a title="Mary Overton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1094533-mary-overton"><img alt="Mary Overton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1208507198p2/1094533.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

  <span id="freeText13600545419891308117" style="display:none">“The Marriage of Heaven and Hell”<br />[…]	If the doors of perception were cleansed, every thing would appear<br />to man as it is: infinite.<br />	For man has closed himself up, till he sees all things thro'<br />narrow chinks of his cavern.<br /><br />[autograph:] William Blake: one who is very much delighted with being in good company. Born 28 Nov 1757 in London &amp; has died several times since.<br /><br />“Auguries of Innocence”<br />[…]<br />Every Night &amp; every Morn<br />Some to Misery are Born<br />Every Morn &amp; every Night<br />Some are Born to sweet delight<br />Some are Born to sweet delight<br />Some are Born to Endless Night<br /><br />[Letter to Mr. Butts, Felpham, August 16, 1803]<br />O, why was I born with a different face?<br />Why was I not born like the rest of my race?<br />When I look, each one starts; when I speak, I offend;<br />Then I'm silent &amp; pensive, &amp; I lose every Friend.<br /><br />Then my verse I dishonour, My pictures despise;<br />My person degrade, &amp; my temper chastise;<br />And the pen is my terror, the pencil my shame;<br />All my talents I bury, and dead is my Fame.<br /><br />I am either too low, or too highly priz'd;<br />When Elate I'm Envy'd, When Meek I'm despis'd.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1900755468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3611361744195235103">Blake was one of the first poets I ever read and have come across time and time again and is also one of the only poets I've ever studied whose works I've thoroughly enjoyed reading. Thought provoking and full of interesting discourses - a sobering but refreshing step back from the typical 18th Century Gothic or Austen novel. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2609355845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2609355845" />
    <a title="Jay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/79948120-jay"><img alt="Jay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522193277p2/79948120.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2609355845?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jay" href="/user/show/79948120-jay">Jay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2609355845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10266594276091443198">William Blake, on his birthday November 28<br/> "I must create a system or be enslaved by another man’s.” William Blake<br/> Satires of both Church and State authority, radical freedom and a revolutionary engagement with aristocratic tyranny and the English monarchy, a visionary theology which privileges direct personal insight without a filtering system of priests or the inquisitors and crusaders who enforce their rule, together with a mature poetics which abandons rhyme and meter and an art of storytell</span>
  <span id="freeText10266594276091443198" style="display:none">William Blake, on his birthday November 28<br /> "I must create a system or be enslaved by another man’s.” William Blake<br /> Satires of both Church and State authority, radical freedom and a revolutionary engagement with aristocratic tyranny and the English monarchy, a visionary theology which privileges direct personal insight without a filtering system of priests or the inquisitors and crusaders who enforce their rule, together with a mature poetics which abandons rhyme and meter and an art of storytelling without character or plot, William Blake was a iconoclast akin to Nietzsche.<br /> His poetry takes flight from Milton's Paradise Lost and the great theme of rebellion, from Dante and his transformative journey of initiation and hierarchial system of symbols and images, and his own reinterpretation of the Bible as an ur-source. Whitmanesque free verse and Joycean Modernism here collide in full glory long before these literary descendants would shock the world with their reinvention. William Blake was a man out of time; his vision at least was unbound by it.<br /> As an author-illustrator his visual art reminds me of Salvador Dali, though grounded in the techniques which would be employed a generation later by Delacroix, and the illustrations of medieval books of magic. Indeed when I first read Blake while in high school I mistook his works for those of a magician writing in the tradition of the grimoires; they are very similar but in fact Blake's ideas are independently arrived at and conceptually original. His is the great rebellion against neoclassical conventions which helped ignite Romanticism, rather than the transmission of a secret wisdom of antiquity. This is not to say he was unaware of such esotericisms, merely that his own were original, results of an immense Biblical scholarship and referential to its symbolism. <br /> An anarchist and forerunner of Marx who fully supported the French Revolution and its project of liberating all mankind from enslavement to the rule of kings and the creation of a free society of equals, as well as its anticolonialist mirror in America, William Blake waged class war through his ideological subversions of the power structures he characterized as demonic and forces of spiritual death. There are echoes of his great project in both Existentialism and Surrealism.<br /> Twin of his revolutionary counterpart de Sade, who together face Janus-like to the past and the future, the inner and outer worlds, the personas we show to others and those we hold secret unto ourselves, Blake the Dionysian occult poet and Platonic Idealist horrified at what Schiller called the disgodding of nature and fighting for the resacralization of the world as if bearing like Orpheus the souls of all humanity from the abyss, and de Sade the Apollonian rationalist whose echoes can be heard in Freud, Marx, and Sartre saying there's nothing hidden inside us, and the abominations of history are perpetrated by those who pretend otherwise and who must be overthrown. <br /> As always, the forces of Conservatism and of Revolution in dynamic balance, shaping us over time as history, memory, and identity, complex and nuanced, highly contingent and volatile, creative and growth directed, shifting in context and meaning and unpredictable. A hundred years and more later, the Romantics would play the Conservative side of the board against their spiritual descendants; a hundred years from now, who shall rebel against us?<br /> His embrace of imagination, dreams and visions, as the true path to the realization of the Ideal, and not that of reason and empirical studies of nature, aligns him with Coleridge and the other English Romantics. His pursuit of creative truth led to the writing of an antiauthoritarian counter-narrative to the Bible, a new and unique mythology crafted in a series of masterpieces which include Milton, Vala or the Four Zoas, Jerusalem, and the preliminary works The Book of Urizen and The Marriage of Heaven and Hell.<br /> These beautiful and grand evocations of dreams and raptures are the equals of any writing sacred or profane in terms of quality as literature, and find expression in the hallucinogenic and fantastical works of Alfred Jarry, Antonin Artaud, William S. Burroughs, Djuna Barnes, Leonora Carrington, and Jonathan Carroll, and kindred spirits in Ryunosuke Akutagawa, Mircea Cartarescu, or anyone else who's gone down the rabbit hole and returned bearing witness to things strange and new.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10266594276091443198" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_3188034909" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3188034909" />
    <a title="JP" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8261480-jp"><img alt="JP" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1418288025p2/8261480.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3188034909?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JP" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JP" href="/user/show/8261480-jp">JP</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8261480-jp?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8261480-jp?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3188034909" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer702156335372699038">A long time ago (in high school), I was told by a teacher that he liked my poetry. It reminded him of William Blake. It's the sort of comment that sticks with you. For me, I still remember it from time to time a decade and change later. It's been a while since I've read any Blake, so perhaps it's time for another go. <br/><br/>Throughout the Complete Works, there are essentially three parts to Blake's writing:<br/><br/>- Philosophical writings on the nature of religion and the universe<br/>- Straight random poetry in So</span>
  <span id="freeText702156335372699038" style="display:none">A long time ago (in high school), I was told by a teacher that he liked my poetry. It reminded him of William Blake. It's the sort of comment that sticks with you. For me, I still remember it from time to time a decade and change later. It's been a while since I've read any Blake, so perhaps it's time for another go. <br /><br />Throughout the Complete Works, there are essentially three parts to Blake's writing:<br /><br />- Philosophical writings on the nature of religion and the universe<br />- Straight random poetry in Songs of Innocence and Experience<br />- A looong collection of rambling poems detailing a strange fantastic mythology of angels, demons, and all sorts of other beings that seems to go on and on<br /><br />The first is fascinating. Blake has quite a way with words:<br /><br /><blockquote><br /><b>Principle 1</b>: That the Poetic Genius is the True Man, and that the Body or Outward Form of Man is derived from the Poetic Genius. Likewise that the FOrms of all things are derived from their Genius, which by the Ancients was call'd an Angel and Spirit and Demon. <br /><br /><b>Principle 2</b>: As all men are alike in Outward Form; so, and with the same infinite variety, all are alike in the poetic Genius. <br /><br />...<br /><br /><b>Principle 4</b>: As none travelling over known lands can find out the unknown; so, from already acquired knowledge, Man could not acquire more, therefor an universal Poetic Genius exists.<br /><br /><b>Principle 5</b>: The Religions of all Nations are derived from each Nation's different reception of the Poetic Genius, which is everywhere call'd the Spirit of Prophecy.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />I like it. It's weird, but I see echos of my own personal beliefs in there. <br /><br />The second is what most people think of when they think of Blake. I really do recommend that everyone give Songs of Innocence and Experience a read when they get a chance. And find an illustrated version. The works and illustrations were both done by Blake and make more of a feel. <br /><br />My favorites:<br />- A Cradle Song<br />- Night<br />- Spring<br />- A Dream<br />- On Anothers Sorrow<br />- Earth's Answer<br />- The Tyger<br />- My Pretty Rose Tree<br />- Infant Sorrow<br />- A Poison Tree<br /><br /><blockquote><br /><b>Infant Sorrow</b><br /><br />My mother groand! my father wept. <br />Into the dangerous world I leapt:<br />Helpless, naked, piping loud;<br />Like a fiend hid in a cloud.<br /><br />Struggling in my fathers hands:<br />Striving against my swadling bands:<br />Bound and weary I thought best<br />To sulk upon my mothers breast.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />Sounds so familiar. <br /><br />In a lot of these, I see what that high school teacher meant. They are familiar to what I'd written and before I'd ever read Blake. It makes me want to write once more. There's just something about reading and poetry. It lets you see the beauty in words and the world. <br /><br />The third part, the weird mythology... has a lot of beautiful and terrible imagery. <br /><br /><blockquote><br />3: Effluvia vapor'd above<br />In noxious clouds; these hover'd thick<br />Over the disorganiz'd Immortal,<br />Till petrific pain scurfd o'er the Lakes<br />As the bones of man, solid &amp; dark<br /></blockquote><br /><br />Blake has quite a way with words.<br /><br />That being said, they just go on and on. Read a few pages. If you like it, there's lots more. If you find yourself skipping ahead, just give it up. It doesn't really change. Honestly, I skipped large parts. So it goes. <br /><br />Overall, worth the read. It's an interesting contrast to what I normally read. If you give it a try, make sure you look at the illustrated versions. So very wonderfully weird. </span>
  <a data-text-id="702156335372699038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10731220373628470723">Absolutely the greatest book I have ever read. Blake's poetry and prose are unmatched, whether he's invoking life and nature, questioning religion (especially fundamentalism), or calling a fundamentalist reverend on his hypocrisy, this book has it all. <br/><br/>I will never remove this book from my collection, and I'm still waiting to see if anything knocks it off #1 on my all-time favorites list. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RGIjUm0lSt0q8mh/DzYtSCGZV6k4lmUogLn4Kvw9wGISATlp9O3ZwrV2I2E6U44i2HLAnpXc3cxfqXgF04/9ow==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('d9z4UWW2YJouKy1h0V/+6c1xhxhMIqMuQQ5CT4ZSJ0Mhv+Jq/H7zhbGvZn/kOl2DNJoQL+FoG8qeHsJgqeAagg==')})
  }

Not until the 1955 publication of The Complete Poems of Emily Dickinson, a three-volume critical edition compiled by Thomas H. Johnson, were readers able for the first time to assess, understand, and appreciate the whole of Dickinson’s extraordinary poetic genius.\n\nThis book, a distillation of the three-volume Complete It contains one hundred of Cummings’s wittiest and most profound poems, harvested from thirty-five of the most radically creative years in contemporary American poetry. These poems exhibit all the extraordinary lyricism, playfulness, technical ingenuity, and compassion for which Cummings is famous. The authoritative texts are based on the Cambridge Tennyson; additional selections have been taken from Sir Charles Tennyson\'s editions of Tennyson\'s Unpublished Early Poems (1931) and The Devil and the Lady (1930), as well as the Eversley edition, with notes by the poet\'s son. The texts of the poems are copiously annotated and the lines of poetry conveniently numbered for easy reference. A special section, Juvenilia and Early Responses, offers easy access to work by the young Tennyson, not readily available elsewhere, together with responses from his contemporaries.  Criticism includes significant statements on Tennyson as well as interpretations of the major poems. A special feature is Georg Roppen\'s essay on Tennyson and the theory of evolution. Other critical voices are those of A. C. Bradley, Harold Nicolson, Douglas Bush, Arthur J. Carr, T. S. Eliot, Paull F. Baum, John Killham, F. E. L. Priestley, Francis Golffing, and Robert W.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13453.William_Blake">About William Blake</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/13453.William_Blake"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1199069675p3/13453.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/13453.William_Blake">William Blake
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,451
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer321751891347872765">William Blake was an English poet, painter, and printmaker. Largely unrecognised during his lifetime, Blake's work is today considered seminal and significant in the history of both poetry and the visual arts.<br/><br/>Blake's prophetic poetry has been said to form "what is in proportion to its merits the least read body of poetry in the language". His visual artistry has led one modern critic to proclaim h</span>
  <span id="freeText321751891347872765" style="display:none">William Blake was an English poet, painter, and printmaker. Largely unrecognised during his lifetime, Blake's work is today considered seminal and significant in the history of both poetry and the visual arts.<br /><br />Blake's prophetic poetry has been said to form "what is in proportion to its merits the least read body of poetry in the language". His visual artistry has led one modern critic to proclaim him "far and away the greatest artist Britain has ever produced." Although he only once travelled any further than a day's walk outside London over the course of his life, his creative vision engendered a diverse and symbolically rich corpus, which embraced 'imagination' as "the body of God", or "Human existence itself".<br /><br />Once considered mad for his idiosyncratic views, Blake is highly regarded today for his expressiveness and creativity, and the philosophical and mystical currents that underlie his work. His work has been characterized as part of the Romantic movement, or even "Pre-Romantic", for its largely having appeared in the 18th century. Reverent of the Bible but hostile to the established Church, Blake was influenced by the ideals and ambitions of the French and American revolutions, as well as by such thinkers as Emanuel Swedenborg.<br /><br />Despite these known influences, the originality and singularity of Blake's work make it difficult to classify. One 19th century scholar characterised Blake as a "glorious luminary", "a man not forestalled by predecessors, nor to be classed with contemporaries, nor to be replaced by known or readily surmisable successors."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="321751891347872765" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
